Overall it is a well-produced, well-written, highly-addictive collection of songs that achieve their goal. Love Into The Light and Past Lives even slow things down and provide atmosphere and a bit of emotional depth, as well.  Really, the only misstep is the pseudo-Taylor-Swift-pop-country tune Wonderland, which just sounds too manufactured, too blatant an attempt at crossover potential to be convincing.<br /><br />If Madonna or Lady Gaga had a sense of humor and didn't take themselves so seriously, they might be able to make music this fun.  After all, it's pop music, folks, not bilateral international arms negotiations.  In the end, I doubt anyone who enjoyed her previous work will be let down by this release at all and there are enough potential singles on here to keep Ke$ha on the airwaves for the next year or so.

B000OZ0NXA|3.0|I had read two other books in the Reacher series before picking up this, the first in the series. I'd enjoyed the previous two enough to read more but not so much that I raced out with the intent of reading them all or of recommending them to my novel-reading friends. And so I came to Killing Floor with hopeful curiosity. I was hoping to get a better handle on who this guy Jack Reacher really was, how his travels began, what made him who he had become. And as I began reading, I got just this. I loved the first several chapters. I did get a feel for Reacher, this former military cop that, after being released from service, decided to take to the road and simply live as a free spirit. As a child, he'd been a military brat, moving from base to base, often country to country. He had no roots to come home to. There was no place he could begin to call home. I also enjoyed the concept of Reacher exiting the bus, on a whim, in the town of Margrave, Georgia, to learn what had really happened to a blues musician that had gone missing decades ago. Unfortunately, Reacher  is almost immediately picked up for a murder he didn't commit.  And thus begins the story proper. I really did thoroughly enjoy the first third or so of the book. But the further I got into it, the more I was required to suspend my disbelief. Police simply wouldn't let a wandering outsider direct their murder investigation, they certainly wouldn't essentially offer wordless shrugs when he kills people - bad people, yes, but still, he kills them. These are cops after all, and by this point in the story they've only known Reacher for a handful of days. As well, I'm never fond of a character that has all of the answers and everyone else simply nods their heads offering no additional help or insight. These supposedly experienced detectives follow Reacher around like puppies on a leash. There are some good twists. Some great thrills. A decent love relationship. But Reacher is the only character with any true depth.I enjoyed the story. It was a finally crafted plot with plenty of twists and turns and reveals. But it's peopled with one dimensional stick figures. I wanted to love it more than I did. I really wanted to jump fully into the Reacher camp and walk away wanting more. And likely I will read more. But they'll be spaced out months, perhaps years apart. I'll enjoy the occasional visit with Reacher, but we won't become close. Maybe I'm a bit of a wanderer too.

B0041KVV8K|5.0|(all music links are to vintage LPs)<br /><br />Of the three compilations released in America while the Beatles were actively recording, (\\"[[ASIN:B000FHDSN4 Yesterday & Today]],\\" \\"[[ASIN:B000RTSDKI Yellow Submarine]]\\" and \\"[[ASIN:B000TBI40I The Beatles Again - Hey Jude]]\\"), YELLOW SUBMARINE was and is the most universally dismissed. Yet, this record truly deserves a fair shake, for it has more than its share of quality rock and orchestral music.<br /><br />Side One of the album is absolutely solid. Yes, the title cut and \\"All You Need Is Love\\" are retreads-- but they're GOOD ones! \\"Hey Bulldog\\" stands among the unit's best recordings, as an expression of joy and a work of solidarity. \\"All Together Now,\\" with its odd noises and pure enthusiasm never fails to bring a smile. \\"It's All Too Much\\" can be hypnotic and you strain to hear everything as it slowly fades away (\\"MUCH too MUCH too MUCH too MUCH...TO-OO much TO-OO much TO-OO much...\\").<br /><br />This leaves the record's masterpiece, George Harrison's brilliant exploration into compression and out-of-phase sound, \\"Only A Northern Song.\\" Originally intended for [[ASIN:B000MU4F5Y SGT. PEPPER'S LONELY HEARTS CLUB BAND]], this swirling, disorienting track has to be heard through quality headphones (\\"cans\\") to be fully appreciated. (PS-- don't let anyone convince you that the \\"remastered\\" CD version which stripped away all the original's special effects, or this 2012 LP MONAURAL take are superior, cuz they ain't!)<br /><br />Now, on to the record's (dreaded) second side-- George Martin's moment in the sun, as it were. The instrumentals vividly call to mind moments from the animated [[ASIN:B00000JRUQ YELLOW SUBMARINE]] (1968) feature they were extracted from. \\"Pepperland\\" stands on its own as a superb recording, whiile the meandering \\"Sea of Time\\" and \\"Sea of Holes\\" are gently psychedelic-- 4 am music, perhaps. \\"Sea of Monsters\\" is indeed an aural nightmare. \\"March of the Meanies\\" has an effectively sinister air and \\"Pepperland Laid Waste\\" draws forth mental images of that magical place in ruins. 0525423656|5.0|Matched is one of my favorite Dystopian reads of all time, so I was very excited to read its sequel Crossed. Crossed is told from both Cassia's and Ky's POV. I felt this was very interesting and helped me get a better understand of Ky and why he's so reluctant about certain things. I also liked the read the conflicting things both Cassia and Ky think of each other, it helped me understand their love for each other and get down to the root of it. Crossed starts where Matched left off, Cassia sorted Ky into the group of men that were sent to the Outer Provinces to fight for the society. Cassia having realized her mistake and that this is basically a death sentence for Ky goes looking for him.<br /><br />I'm going to be quite frank here; I'm having mixed emotions about this book. Scratch that, I think the book was good I'm having mixed emotions at how little Ally choose to include Xander in this book. Excuse me while I rant...<br />When I first read Matched I was leaning towards Cassia ending up with Xander, even though I myself loved Ky there was something about the way Xander loved Cassia that made me want them to be together. In crossed the story is told in both Cassia's and Ky's POV, I found this both entertaining and annoying. Xander is only in the first few pages of the book which really annoyed me, I wanted to get to read about him more, Ally includes him in little things he leaves for Cassia but it still annoyed me, he should have been in the book more. While we do learn something completely mind blowing about Xander in this book I couldn't help but feel Xander never really got a chance to have the girl.<br /><br />In my opinion, Crossed suffered a bit from second book syndrome but not a lot. My biggest and really only problem with this book was how little Xander is in the book but other than that I found it quite enjoyable. Cassia and Ky's love proves to grow stronger throughout the series and the many obstacles they face both inside the society and in the outer provinces. I found the use of the story being told in Ky's POV help me lean a bit towards my favor of him ending up with Cassia but I'm still more team Xander.<br /><br />One of the things I loved most about Matched other than it's complete originally was how Ally's writing style was very poetic in its self. It was very beautiful. I felt that in crossed where were only a few moments when we got to see that again though. Crossed also features a set of new characters, Vick, Eli and Indie. All three contributed in making the story what it was and I felt Ally couldn't have created better characters for the people Cassia and Ky meet in the outer provinces. (SPOILER ALER) Although if I'm being frank I found myself not liking Indie all that much, it probably had to do with her liking Xander randomly.

If you were particularly attached to Sabriel and Touchstone, you may have to adjust to the new characters, but you can do it. I believe in you.<br /><br />\\"Lirael\\" is a 'coming of age' story for multiple characters. As such, it is rife with passages of self-doubt, moodiness, and denial, particularly in the first third of the novel. Sameth, a secondary hero and companion to the titular Lirael, is also trying to find his place in the world after his Abhorsen mother begins to call him the Abhorsen-in-Waiting.<br /><br />Many reviews complain about the early portion of Sameth's journey, which gets a bit of a slow start. The tale does move slowly at first, before all of the characters have been united, and Sam's beginning is the most difficult portion of the Abhorsen trilogy to slog through. Lirael's journey through the Library of the Clayr can keep you hooked, and if you struggle through Sam's portion, just remember that you have Lirael's portion to look forward to in the next chapter.<br /><br />Lirael's humanity, her doubts and flaws in self-image, resonate with a reader who has felt dejection and exclusion. Lirael's relationship with the Dog is so real and touching; the reader desires a companionship so strong and honest. It is the scenes between Lirael and her best friend that give \\"Lirael\\" her heart. The Dog is one of the most charming, loveable characters I have ever read.<br /><br />If you have read \\"Sabriel,\\" \\"Lirael\\" is a must-read. If you are new to the Abhorsen trilogy, start with \\"Sabriel\\" and see if you like it.

0060935464|5.0|\\"To Kill a Mockingbird\\" is one of the best books I have ever read. It is a literary classic on many levels. The book was a landmark work written in the context of the burgeoning civil rights movement. Harper Lee was a born and bred Southerner and she provided detailed descriptions of what is was like to grow up in the South. Her analysis was not autobiographical, but she clearly explained the peculiar mores, religion, academic institutions, and social norms of the era. Her detailed descriptions had a great deal of authenticity.<br /><br />She described the pervasive institutional racism of 1930's Maycomb County, Alabama. Tom Robinson, a black man, was falsely accused of raping a white girl, Mayella Ewell. Atticus Finch was the lawyer who defended Robinson at the trial. Robinson was convicted despite spurious evidence and contradictory, inaccurate testimony on the part of the defendants. Scout and Jem finch were Tom's children. The story is written from Scout's perspective. She was a young grade school girl who came of age. Scout and her brother were forced to learn the sad reality of inequality and racism of the time.<br /><br />Lee was a talented wordsmith who described the setting and characters with intricate details. She was able to maintain suspense throughout the novel, especially regarding the recluse neighbor Arthur \\"Boo\\" Radley. The story was always coherent and even minor characters in the book were memorable.<br /><br />In short, this book was a masterpiece of the highest order. It is a compelling tale from a narrative perspective. The social implications of the book are as relevant in the present day as when it was written. This is a tale about how children come to the realization that the world is not always fair and there are people who are victims of discrimination and violence. This is a phenomenal book, one of the very best and most significant novels I have ever read.

0316066524|5.0|This is one of those rare books that demands that you ignore the reviews.  Yes, I realize that I'm writing one, but trust me on this -- no one can do justice to the experience of reading Infinite Jest without actually reading the book (like comedy, and a frog, it tends to die when dissected).  So what should you know about this book, then, in order to decide whether to read it?  Do you enjoy reading books where the footnotes tell the critical parts of the story?  Do you enjoy books that are occasionally lucid tracts on addiction and at other times bizarrely comic slams on American consumerism? Do you like with Quebecois sepratism?  How about tennis?<br /><br />Sigh.<br /><br />You see, it just isn't possible to turn this into a linear narrative worth \\"explaining\\" and everyone who tries inevitably makes it seem boring, foolish or pretentious or some combination of all three.  Instead, think about it this way: Infinite Jest is a tour of the mind of brilliant polymath who desperately wanted to understand what it was to be happy or fulfilled when he could never do it himself, and the addictive ways we fool ourselves into thinking that we are actually happy.<br /><br />Random detail: I interviewed DFW when Infinite Jest came out, and he was a charming, self-effacing man with a keen eye for the absurd and so much self-awareness and minutae crammed into his brain that it must have been debilitating (he was like the narrator of the Borges story, Funes The Memorious, about a man who never forgets anything).  His death was a great loss, and reading Infinite Jest explains why.  That, I guess, is why you should read the book -- to understand how a great American writer could find America and still be so lost.
B0054U56B4|3.0|The sophomore slump definitely hits Gatlin, South Caroina in the second installment of the (generally) wonderful Caster Chronicles.  The first book in the series, Beautiful Creatures, was a fantastic effort to bring classic southern gothic tropes to a more traditional YA supernatural thriller/love story.  It even had a touch of literary aspiration, in the occasional echoes of Jeffrey Eugenides and the Virgin Suicides, and even Faulkner.  It wasn't perfect, but it was a great read and enormously promising.  Beautiful Darkness, by contrast, is still a great read, and still fun, but it lacks the resonance of the first book, and often resorts to some pretty lame genre cliches in order to move the story along.  No spoilers, but what had seemed like a simple story of a young woman forced to chose between the light and dark sides of herself (narrated, intriguingly, by her boyfriend -- and the male perspective on a story like this was quite clever), has turned into a rehash of many of the storylines of the Harry Potter story.  This is a story that works best when it isn't trying to save the world, but is instead trying to show us teenagers learning how to understand their own place in the world.  Still worth reading (I'm starting the third book now) but feeling a bit nervous that the story will trend more towards Twilight and less towards The Night Circus.  We'll see.

0307887448|4.0|Won't get into the details. Here's a list of all the overarching factors that I think come into making a book either good or not:<br /><br />Voice/Narrative: First person POV. Distinctive, believable and consistent throughout the story. The main character doesn't get lost into too much exposition, but he doesn't assume too much either--which is important in Sci-fi! Though some parts seemed rushed, the narrative did a good job of maximizing the appropriate effect in the various scenes and events of the story. 4/5.<br /><br />Plot/Storyline: Unique and extremely specific. If you didn't grow up in the 80's, make sure you keep close to your laptop when you crack this bad boy open. Believable and legitimate turns of events, ending wasn't obvious nor was it totally oblique either. Kept my interest throughout all 300+ pages. Never loses pace. 4/5.<br /><br />Characters/Setting: The setting for me is just another character in the story and here, I think, is where the book is at its best. Both the world and characters in Ready Player One are, in a word, sublime. Complex, charming, and just plain awesome! And if your wondering which do I mean here, the world or the characters--I mean both. 5/5.<br /><br />Last but not least...<br /><br />Figura/Symbolism: This I attribute to the philosophical issues a book addresses or brings to light. Those things that make us stop reading a moment and tilt our heads slightly the left. Throughout the book, I thought I could detect a subtle hint of something. It was faint, and neither the characters nor plot acknowledge the issues they seemed to dance around. This is understandable, since I assume that was not what the author was intending to accomplish when he wrote this book. Its just not that kind of novel, bro. Still, theres a little something there, and though those of you who like to question gravity would probably be itching for a more explicit approach of these issues, just know that the rest of the story will more then make up for it. 3/5.<br /><br />Overall, as my rating obviously displays, this is a definite must read.<br /><br />Also, if it means anything, theres a movie on the way!!<br /><br />This has been Malek, over-and-out.

B003E88TKS|3.0|George Clooney plays a man whose job it is to travel the country firing people. If the complexities of that issue - the stress of having such a role, the pathos experienced by those thus fired, the perversity of the corporations who needed to hire outside help to perform that function - were investigated, this could have been a very interesting film. Instead, the story is primarily about a man who, by having opted for a job in which he spends more time traveling than at home, pursues his two primary goals: that of avoiding any deep human connections (we're treated to lectures he gives in which he encourages audience members to imagine that a backpack - placed conveniently next to him at his lectern - contains all of the important people in their lives. &#34;Now imagine lifting it,&#34; he says), and of achieving 10,000,000 frequent flyer miles. We watch as he (almost) gives up on the former, and achieves the latter. While the manner in which those milestones occur are certainly very entertaining - and worth watching the film for, if you're in the mood for light fare - they're certainly not treated with any depth. There is much more reality and human drama in the brief segments in which people react to having just been fired. I wish the film had spent more time on those, and less time on the significantly less substantial &#34;main&#34; story.

0545265355|1.0|I usually don't bother with reviews. But I am bothering for these books. I think I hated them more than Twilight. At least Twilight does not decimate (either physically or mentally) every character you love in the series (or just plain every character in the books period). While these books are substantially better written and, I will be honest, are page-turners in that you want desperately to find out what happens, it is not worth what you go through as a reader to find out. These books delve into the depths of morality, virtue, and humanity while making a distinct and easily understood commentary on what our society could someday become (but hopefully, others would stand with me to prevent this type of travesty from ever happening). But they do so in a way that is depressing, brutally graphic, cold and psychologically disturbing. The horror faced by the characters in this series definitely states that no one comes through life unscathed but the lengths to which this point is driving home are unnecessary.  The author has to be twisted in some way to be as calculatingly cruel as she is to her characters, and through them, her readers. I would *NOT LET* a teen read them, let alone any younger child. And I will not be recommending these disturbing books to anyone else. They may have a place in the history of literature for their commentary on society, morality and ethics but they do not belong in the hands of children so that they can read about the slaying of characters that could, if real, be their peers...or, worse, themselves. Don't listen to the hype and ignore the movie. Skip over these books unless you are an adult who enjoys sorrow and morbid depression. They are not \\"feel good\\" in any way, shape, or form. 1936891026|4.0|This book has a simple message. The war referred to in the title is between Resistance and Creativity. The author, Steven Pressfield lays out a simple premise. Resistance (the capitalization is deliberate) is an impersonal, implacable foe that fights our creative drive or urges. It's impersonal in that it inflicts itself on everyone, and in direct relation our personal need to create. Creativity doesn't just mean painting or writing, but can be any activity that we are compelled to undertake, like dieting or developing a deep relationship. The stronger the personal need to do something the stronger resistance will be. In other words, the greater the fear the greater our personal need to do something.<br /><br />When I say do something, this book is all about taking action. That action may be sitting down in front of keyboard for so many hours or pages (daily-we never beat Resistance). It may be practicing an instrument. It may be counting calories eaten or miles run. Resistance moves to stop us in either direct or indirect ways. Avoidance, procrastination, rationalization, letting the creative time slip, just this once. You get the idea.<br /><br />The opposite urge is the one to create. Call it your muse or angel, or Creativity, it, also, is impersonal, rewarding us in direct relation to the amount of effort we give towards our personal creative urges. Outcomes aren't what creativity is about, nor are fame or fortune. The creative act is its own reward.  Pros face Resistance directly and do the work. When they do, Creativity steps in and allows the creative urge or muse to work through us. It works individually without the need for therapy, support or process, at least according to Mr. Pressfield, who has obviously practice what he preaches.<br /><br />That's pretty much the whole book. It reads like a Seth Godin manifesto. Things are laid out in black and white. You're either giving in to Resistance or worshiping at the alter of Creativity. If you respond well to this kind of book, it's great. If you want a more nuanced exploration there are other works like: [[ASIN:0393311066 The Courage to Create]] by Rollo May or [[ASIN:0609803700 Callings: Finding and Following an Authentic Life]] by Greg Levoy. Another wise work is [[ASIN:B002WGPM3K Art & Fear]]  by Bayles & Orland. I found The War of Art a good kick in the pants, which makes it well worth the cost.this kind of book, it's great. If you want a more nuanced exploration there are other works like: [[ASIN:0393311066 The Courage to Create]] by Rollo May or [[ASIN:0609803700 Callings: Finding and Following an Authentic Life]] by Greg Levoy. Another wise work is [[ASIN:B002WGPM3K Art & Fear]]  by Bayles & Orland. I found The War of Art a good kick in the pants, which makes it well worth the cost.

0061474355|5.0|I think one of the reasons this series and Sabriel in particular has such a draw is the almost Tolkienian sense of loss that the reader feels early on--the Old Kingdom has fallen into quasi-anarchy without the royal family to lead it or mend what's been broken (trying not to spoil here), and on a more personal level Sabriel's own memories of the Old Kingdom have lost since faded away. As to Sabriel's character, I enjoy that she is not just another hyper-reactive heroine: throughout the book she tends to approach situations almost academically--which keeps with her bookish, proper character--rather than simply *feeling* things all the time, which is a common pitfall with female characters. I've seen this criticized a few times, but I think it allows the reader to superimpose their own feelings onto hers--I enjoy being able to put myself in her shoes. This is less the case with Lirael, which the bulk of the series deals with: by this point, Nix has hit his stride and has much more room to work in with the duology--gut feelings and emotional involvement have a much larger impact on the story itself, whereas in Sabriel there is simply a job to be done.<br /><br />Possibly the best thing in Nix's writing, however, is that there is very little 'fluff' to it, despite the fact that in Lirael the meat of the plot takes a while to become obvious. Almost every dialogue means something to the plot, lends itself greatly to character development, and moves the story along, which might have been difficult given how much more emotion is present in this part of the series. The emotional draw of character development is much stronger with Lirael, as well, as the story starts when she is younger and shows her progress over the next few years. By contrast, Sabriel is almost grown and presumably set in her ways. I enjoy the interplay of similarities and differences between Lirael and Sabriel, which eventually leads the reader to Lirael's inheritance as the Abhorsen-in-waiting.<br /><br />Overall, I've re-read this series a few times now and I recommend it to anyone with ears and an inclination toward fantasy. The plot and the world are tightly written, and the pacing is excellent all the way through. I'll keep reading these books every year for a long while. :)/>Overall, I've re-read this series a few times now and I recommend it to anyone with ears and an inclination toward fantasy. The plot and the world are tightly written, and the pacing is excellent all the way through. I'll keep reading these books every year for a long while. :)

B006PTL1QM|4.0|... ultimately not as profound an investigation into the characters of Sigmund Freud and Carl Jung as it ought to have been, and indeed as it seemed it would be for 90% of the film. It's scarcely a film about Freud at all; Jung is the central character throughout. It's the ethical, emotional and intellectual turmoil of his life that shapes the drama; his divergence from Freud is presented as largely the result of sexual and professional jealousy on both sides, with just the merest hint of the impending catastrophe of anti-semitism and an even slimmer hint of the substantial incompatibility of Freud's and Jung's psychological models. Nevertheless, the very capable acting of Viggo Mortensen as Freud and Michael Fassbender as Jung keeps the film engrossing.<br /><br />The insurmountable problem is the acting of Keira Knightley in the role of the historical person Sabina Spielrein, first shown on screen as a flailing hysteric, then as a challenging patient significantly \\"helped\\" by Jung's psychoanalysis, then as the masochistic lover and manipulator of Jung, thus becoming the \\"wedge\\" between Jung and Freud. The characterological triangle is not entirely implausible. In fact, it might approach \\"fact\\" or at least insight. But Knightley so freakishly overacts throughout that the triangle loses its hypotenuse. Knightley's grimaces and writhings are hard enough to watch, but her spoken dialogue is horrible. She seems to be attempting a \\"Russian\\" accent in this English-language script that rightly should be in German. Alas, her Russo-English slips often - sometimes in mid sentence - into a twangy LA Valley Girl accent. And how's that for weird, since she is actually British by birth? Even if her accent were better managed, her speaking technique is amateurish; \\"stars\\" who are paid celestial salaries surely can be expected to have basic skills of dramatic elocution.<br /><br />I'm stymied about a proper rating for this film. As I said, I found it engrossing; ergo four stars, though Knightly is so awful that I'm tempted to slam the film with a three. Eli the vampire needs a new keeper.  She disposes of the old one and acquires a new one with little fanfare.<br /><br />The neighbors disappear like 5 little indians, with only a limp attempt to fulfill one of the great slasher film cliches, \\"take on the monster alone and die\\".  Both plots are greatly facilitated by the utter lack of police presence in the face of a series of macabre murders.<br /><br />And a revenge fantasy where poor little Oskar finally stands up to the school bullies armed only with a sheath knife and a homicidal vampire bff.  Again, driven by a complete lack of authority figures and some grossly overdrawn delinquents.<br /><br />Since the whole movie centers around Oskar I was sucked into cheering for him and his romance with Eli, then felt completely manipulated when he evolved into the most sociopathic turd of the pile.  It's not clear whether the director intends to condemn vengeance (\\"capital punishment is completely unacceptable in a society of laws\\") in stereotypical Swedish \\"neutrality\\" fashion, or if it's a parody of that, or if he simply wanted to gussy up his vampire trash with some Euro deep thought.  My money's on the latter.<br /><br />Based on reviews by those who read the book, I get the feeling some of the themes got lost in translation.  The neighbors were apparently drunks and perhaps less sympathetic victims than in the film. And the father's drinking problem is so low key that US audiences read the subtext as homosexuality.  Of course, with Jocke and Lacke's mooning about and the nature of Eli's 12 year old immortality, I'm surprised no one has decided this is an extended metaphor about AIDS...<br /><br />To sum up, nothing much of consequence happens, there is no one to cheer for, but it takes two hours for this dismal parade to conclude.

1401224792|4.0|WEREWOLVES OF THE HEARTLAND is the second standalone trade in Bill Willingham's Fables series.  I preferred the first (1001 NIGHTS OF SNOWFALL), but that may be because I loved its structure so much.<br /><br />WEREWOLVES OF THE HEARTLAND finds Bigby Wolf (the Big Bad Wolf to those who haven't read a Fables book before), arriving in Story City, Iowa.  He's searching for a new location for Fabletown and did not expect to find a community of werewolves - especially not one containing a few familiar faces.<br /><br />Vertigo promises that WEREWOLVES OF THE HEARTLAND is a good place to start Fables as well as entertaining adventure for old fans of the mythos.  As someone who has read several Fables books but not even close to half of what is available, I'll endorse that statement.  There's nothing going on in the novel that requires knowledge of the main story arc, and plenty of hints about what has gone on to intrigue new readers.  (At least, it will intrigue new readers who want to see Prince Charming duel Bluebeard.)<br /><br />Art is, of course, a big part of any successful graphic novel.  For the most part I like the art in WEREWOLVES OF THE HEARTLAND.  The colors are soft, the lines are clean, and there are plenty of backgrounds but the panels aren't cluttered.  The shading feels rather old school to me, which I liked.  It fits a tale involving a long flashback to WWII.  But some panels just didn't work for me.  Some of the close ups just have such dead eyes that it is both creepy and distracting.<br /><br />The story is pretty simple.  I wish Oda, the girl passed out on the cover, had more to do.  She might be precognitive and she certainly gets a portentous last line, but she disappears during the climax . . . passed out.  (Before that she gets the cliche woman trying to seduce a guy to her purposes scene.)  Bigby Wolf, meanwhile, gets to show off his sense of law and justice on the frontier.  Unsurprisingly, it's swift and brutal.  It's nice to see a bit of Bigby's past, but it's a bit boring to see him put up against a group of werewolves who are so outclassed.  (And I don't think the whole \\"Bigby-as-the-werewolf-God\\" thing was every fully explained.)<br /><br />WEREWOLVES OF THE HEARTLAND is an entertaining adventure and will definitely appeal to those looking to read a standalone graphic novel in addition to fans of the Fables series.  Fairytale fans might pursue an entry in the series more based in folklore, however.  And yes, this book is rated for mature readers including violence and full-frontal nudity of both genders, mostly nonsexual. 1451695195|4.0|Books on near-death experiences (NDEs) can be both wonderful--in the fullest sense of the term--and frustrating. This is particularly true in the case of Alexander's book.<br /><br />On the one hand the book is very suggestive of the \\"more things in heaven and earth\\" than most people dream of in their philosophies. The plot in a nutshell is this: Dr. Alexander is an experienced and well-published neurosurgeon who had a uniquely horrible encounter with meningitis, from which his chances of survival, let along full recovery, were absurdly low. His neo-cortex was completely \\"shut down\\" for a week, restricting him to reptilian-brain functioning. Yet he survived, and returned inexplicably to full mental functioning. The extreme character of the medical story alone makes this book truly fascinating. But of course it is not simply about this recovery. Alexander awoke with the immediate memory of an excessively realistic, highly complex experience with spiritual-conscious realities he arbitrarily labels the \\"Earthworm's Eye-View,\\" the \\"Gateway,\\" the \\"Core,\\" \\"Om,\\" and so forth. This experience shocked him out of his cozy relationship with materialist reductionism (as a neurosurgeon it is a natural methodological position), and taught him that the universe has a vast invisible dimension of consciousness, in which there is a single truth: love.<br /><br />With that last point, one of the frustrating elements emerges: a sense of hokiness. At various points one hears echoes of this or that religious tradition as Alexander represents his experience, but only insofar as they help him convey a one-tone message of cosmic love. As the \\"Girl on the Butterfly Wing\\" tells him: \\"You are loved and cherished. You have nothing to fear. You can do no wrong\\" (71). The problem of suffering turns out not to be one, since it is a necessary condition for spiritual growth (a potentially substantive argument) and the amount of good in the universe vastly overwhelms the amount of evil (a possible but dissatisfying argument classically articulated by Leibniz) (48, 83). One can't help but feeling a bit leery about such a God. How do we bring it down to earth? Is it too immaterial, too unperturbed, too personal, its message too simple? Not for Alexander, for whom all this has an immediacy--which I can respect--that holds up under reflective nitpicking. But for the rest of us?<br /><br />On the whole I find this and other exceptional NDEs fairly convincing when it comes to challenging the boundaries of reality, even if they create more questions than they answer. Yes, the whole brain was not inactive in Alexander's case, but the possibility of his particular experience stemming from the most archaic parts of the brain is probably just as incredible as the interpretation Alexander gives to it. (His brief appendix of possible neurological explanations and their shortcomings is useful here.)<br /><br />Perhaps the most interesting thing to reflect on here is the place of NDEs and other \\"paranormal\\" events in relation to \\"religion\\" and \\"science,\\" both of which occasionally dislike the paranormal. The reason for this dislike is, of course, that the paranormal challenges set ways of thinking about the world, which we may with good reason hold dearly. Anomalous events are uncomfortable, and we frequently deal with uncomfortable things through ridicule. Humor often masks genuine discomfiture. But at the same time, anomalous events play a central role in both scientific and religious mythology; anomalies can be the portal through which our knowledge of reality can be greatly altered or expanded. Religious and scientific revolutions can be precipitated by one strange fact duly regarded. Religion and science are thus invested in keeping one foot in the world of (radical) empiricism, even though this world threatens to unsettle master narratives. The paranormal is a stubborn reminder of this continuing authority of experience over dogma of every kind. William James is probably still our guide here.<br /><br />The paranormal can also help us break down the rigid divide between the subjective and objective viewpoints, about which Thomas Nagel ([[ASIN:0195056442 The View From Nowhere]]) has written helpfully. One of the real sticking points between \\"religion\\" people and \\"science\\" people (to the extent these artificial tribes exist outside of internet forums) is the validity of the subjective. Is the way things seem to me real in some fundamental sense? Is consciousness important in itself? Does it have its own reality, perhaps indivisible from material reality? Is mind all-pervasive? If so, does it culminate in something like God? Is that culminating reality personal in some way? Are there conscious events that are not merely epiphenomenal shadows of physical ones? Does mind--does my mind--have a destiny that transcends death? These are questions that \\"science\\" people answer more or less in the negative. \\"Religion\\" people generally do not. Now the paranormal suggests, in its weird ways, that the subjective is not just an illusion, a sad little side-effect, an outlier to the \\"really real.\\" If this is true, the values, representations, and goals of consciousness can no longer be dismissed a priori when it comes to addressing the really real. Indeed, certain paranormal experiences grant subjectivity so much reality that their objective content is almost never the same. Jeffrey Kripal ([[ASIN:0226453871 Authors of the Impossible: The Paranormal and the Sacred]]) gives us a good sense of this. Because of the free-form of the subjective, the paranormal by no means provides a blanket confirmation to any particular form of religious representation. On the contrary! Alexander's own experience of the afterlife seems to have disappointed him in certain respects (see pp. 162-64), or at least would have done so had the affective quality of his experience not been so overwhelmingly positive. Those who look on it from without, however, can still feel some consternation. (I did.)<br /><br />In any case, I respect Alexander for writing the book, which is certainly an important one, however it \\"rubs\\" you. Its generic oddness may not help the cause: it is a family drama, a medical record, a paranormal eye-witness account, and a scientific commentary all at woven tightly, but artificially together. In such a small space, it can hardly fulfill all its tasks to everyone's satisfaction, but it does make its principle message clear. I think that more important than any frustration caused by this or that particular should be our wonder at the overall fact of Alexander's experience. It really does make you think, and maybe even hope.

1451626657|5.0|The novel \\"Catch-22\\" was written by Joseph Heller to show how crazy the Air Force made people during World War Two.  The characters are trapped in the Air Force by the catch known as Catch 22, it was meant to stop crazy people from compromising missions by preventing them from flying.  What the catch really did was prevent anybody from being grounded (sent home).  The catch works where if someone was crazy all they had to do was ask somebody to ground them, the only problem was that when they asked to be grounded the catch said that the person was no longer crazy and could not be grounded.  This along with the number of required missions constantly being raised prevents anybody from leaving the Air Force.  Being forced to stay takes its toll on the soldiers as they slowly lose morale and drift into madness.  The main character, Yossarian, realizes that there is no escape other than death, which he, more than anyone, wants to avoid. This prompts him to plot how to avoid going on missions.<br /> Heller writes this story not in any particular chronological order, and instead connects the chapters at random in order to portray the insanity that the characters experience.  The story is analogous to a jig-saw that is given ten pieces at a time.  The reader must remember was has already been said and connect it to what has just been said.  Heller's writing style of using a plethora of ironies and hyperboles adds to the satire of the book, which isn't so much satirizing WW2, but war in general.  His way of writing the character's dialogue also contributed to the insanity that he was trying to portray.  The characters speak in what feels like circles, where the character says what the last character said in a slightly different way.  After a while of this, the conversation loses its original meaning and makes even the reader question his sanity.  The novel effectively satirizes World War Two and possibly all other wars, and conveys themes of power in bureaucracy, the inevitability of death, and a loss of religion.bility of death, and a loss of religion.

0525958002|5.0|I just finished reading Andy Taylor's autobio when I saw that John's book had just been published, so I was able to read them back to back.  As others have mentioned in their reviews, it's hard not to compare/contrast the two.<br /><br />I enjoyed both books, especially because in the nature of being autobios they were conversational.  I prefer autobios over bios because it can feel like the author is speaking to you, which for any fan is a bit of a rush (even though you know it's nothing personal!).<br /><br />JT had a little bit more of a fragmented writing style as far as sentences, mood, and energy go, but in this case it is not at all a bad thing and overall it was very readable and enjoyable.  The chapters are small and numerous, and it generally is a quick but interesting read.<br /><br />He tells us about his seemingly pleasant childhood, and it was kindof fun to learn (by photos and descriptions) that he was a bit on the nerdy side growing up :) Of course, you fairly quickly get to his career with Duran Duran.  You do get some insight to the interplay between the guys, including all the disagreements and such, but he focuses his story mostly on how he himself was dealing with it all...including, of course, his many encounters with the ubiquitous sex and drugs of rock'n'roll.  He is honest, and it is clear that during his heyday he was definitely embodying the idea of being hot, on many levels, and absolutely knowing it.<br /><br />As you make your way through his story, you see the arc of arrogance combined with easy access to anything and everything that culminated in his going to rehab.  You can tell he's still got his ego now, and rightly so as he is still a great musician (and still hot as far as looks goes, in my opinion anyway) but that it has come to a very balanced place.  That of course was helped by going to rehab...a hard decision for someone very skeptical of the rehab process, but bravo that he seems to have stayed sober.  I loved the couple ofchapters describing his rehab experience.<br /><br />As I saw with reviews of Andy's book as well, there are people that may be disappointed that there wasn't more about the infighting between the guys, etc.  John says it himself, late in the book: \\"I'm sorry if I'm being a tease, but it's not my place to go into all the issues and problems we had.  My friendships with all of my bandmates, future and past, are my highest priority.\\"  I admire this, and I think he balances letting the reader in on the ins and outs of the band's relationships without burning bridges or being bitter.  I personally don't need a bunch of dirt on people to find a band's story interesting...but that's just me.  It also seems, though, that he had a little bit better relations with the other guys than Andy did overall anyway...so maybe not as much to dish on from his standpoint. Not that I'm saying that Andy was exceedingly bitter or burned bridges either, but there seemed to be a few more specific interpersonal issues related in detail in his book vs John's.<br /><br />My only disappointment, being that this book was published very recently, is that there wasn't more about the 2000's.  He describes his dad's sad decline and passing, talks a little bit about his marriage to wife Gela, and does go into some detail about the \\"Astronaut\\" album and reunion tour, but it is not very much.  There is nothing about the creative process behind \\"All You Need Is Now,\\" which I really would've liked to read more about because that is SUCH an awesome album. I was hoping for a bit more there. As others have said, the last part of the book seems slightly rushed.<br /><br />With that said, what is lacking in the last decade of the story is made up for in the plethora of photos throughout the book...not just in the expected glossy section (which is awesome!) but at the beginning of each chapter...and there are 74 chapters! John's one of those people that looks really different from photo to photo, even from the same time period, so it's an interesting collection.<br /><br />Overall, loved learning more about John and more about Duran Duran.  I do, however, think it was even better reading BOTH John's and Andy's autobios because what one lacks the other seems to flesh out a bit for the most part.  The two books give more perspective than either one on its own.

1609611543|1.0|When an author gets the basic facts wrong, it puts the author's ideas into the trash. Davis is not an agronomist nor a biochemist, and apparenbtly didn't feel the need to research either before writing his book.<br /><br />Example: In a CBS promo/interview for his book, Davis says: \\"It's an 18-inch tall plant created by genetic research in the '60s and '70s\\"  That was true, and the research was done by Norman Borlaug at CIMMYT, crossing strains of wheat the old-fashioned way to get shorter more productive wheat. Some extremely short varieties were crossed with the taller ones and the descendents with short stems were kept for further crossing. It's the same process that produced the wheat varieties of the 19th, 18th, 17th and earlier centuries.<br /><br />He continues on to say ... \\"This thing has many new features nobody told you about, such as there's a new protein in this thing called gliadin.\\"  ... OMG! A new protein? It's called GLIADIN! We're all going to die.<br /><br />Except that gliadin shows up in scientific literature well before the 1960s. Would you believe the 1830s?<br />In the \\"Penny cyclopaedia of the Society for the Diffusion of Useful Knowledge\\" edited by C. Knight, 1837 one can discover this - \\"Under ordinary circumstances, about a fourth part of what is termed gluten consists of a principle called gliadin.\\"  So that new protein was discovered 175 years ago.

0385343248|4.0|I recently finished this tome and just wanted to share a few thoughts with anyone who's considering tackling this monster for the first time.<br /><br />1.  Yes it's an epic read-between 1,000-1,250 pages depending on which edition you have.  And Clavell creates an amazing world populated with interesting characters, history, and intrigue, BUT...this is not literature, in the traditional sense.  This is more of a popular entertainment barnburner predicated on myth, legend, and many popular stereotypes about Japan during that time in history.<br /><br />2.  Think soap opera drama in 15th Century feudal Japan. This is the strength and weakness of the novel.  The weakness being Clavell will have two characters have a long conversation.  Then one character will go in and eat and you have his thoughts about the meal, and then he'll bathe, and you'll get his thoughts about that, and then he'll prepare for sleep, and you'll get his thoughts about that, but not before said character has another conversation with a servant, or other character.  This passage I described can literally stretch between 20-30 pages.  The strength of this style is the characters have depth, but not enough to get in the way of the story, Clavell knows how to tell a good yarn, and you can literally skp or skim entire sections of the novel and miss nothing of any significance.<br /><br />3.  This book has achieved cult status and rightly so-love it or hate it, Clavell created a vivid world with memorable scenes that will stay with you long after you've finished reading it.  I tried to keep in mind as I read the book that it was written 35 years ago before the internet etc...and these types of books were the hottest show in town.  You have to respect Clavell and the work he poured into creating this epic novel.
